  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_da90e43b-dcc3-4c5e-9dbf-4324144e9189'
      });
    }

      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"

              <span id="reviewTextContainer313336177" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer16762013402540348013"><b>Pulp (novel), Charles Bukowski</b><br/><br/>Pulp is the last completed novel by Los Angeles poet and writer Charles Bukowski. It was published in 1994, shortly before Bukowski's death. <br/><br/>Pulp is a pulp fiction novel which acts also as a meta-pulp. Pulp comments on the obsessions of the pulp fiction genre, making fun of itself as stereotypical of the genre in the grimiest form. <br/><br/>Bukowski dedicates the story to "bad writing", as Bukowski did not plan his mystery novel well and frequently wrote Nicky Belane into ho</span>
  <span id="freeText16762013402540348013" style="display:none"><b>Pulp (novel), Charles Bukowski</b><br /><br />Pulp is the last completed novel by Los Angeles poet and writer Charles Bukowski. It was published in 1994, shortly before Bukowski's death. <br /><br />Pulp is a pulp fiction novel which acts also as a meta-pulp. Pulp comments on the obsessions of the pulp fiction genre, making fun of itself as stereotypical of the genre in the grimiest form. <br /><br />Bukowski dedicates the story to "bad writing", as Bukowski did not plan his mystery novel well and frequently wrote Nicky Belane into holes from which he could not escape. <br /><br />Bukowski wrote some of his most violent, cynical, sarcastic, and shocking work during the final months of his life. Many critics have agreed this novel exemplifies Bukowski showing an acceptance of his own pending mortality.<br /><br />تاریخ نخستین خوانش: روز بیستم ماه ژوئن سال 2010میلادی<br /><br />عنوان: عامه‌ پسند؛ نویسنده: چارلز بوکوفسکی؛ مترجم: پیمان خاکسار؛ مشخصات نشر تهران، نشر چشمه، 1388، در 198ص، شابک 9789643625726؛ چاپ سوم 1389؛ چاپ پنجم 1390؛ چاپ ششم 1393؛ موضوع: داستانهای نویسندگان امریکایی - سده 20م<br /><br />عنوان: عامه‌ پسند؛ نویسنده: چارلز بوکوفسکی؛ مترجم: محمدصادق سبط الشیخ؛ مشخصات نشر تهران، چلچله، 1394، در 332ص،؛ شابک 9789646553835؛ کتابنامه دارد<br /><br />راوی داستان، «نیکی بلان - کارآگاه خصوصی» است؛ که کوشش می‌کند، درباره ی نویسنده ی کلاسیک «فرانسوی» «سلین»، و «گنجشک قرمز» پژوهش کند؛ کنکاش «نیکی بلان»، او را گام به گام، در زندگی سطح پایین شهری فرومی‌برد؛ چیزی که در دیگر نوشته‌ های «بوکوفسکی» نیز موضوعی طبیعی است؛ کتاب با دارا بودن رگه‌ هایی از نوشته‌ های علمی تخیلی، و فوق طبیعی، توسط خود «بوکوفسکی» به «بد نوشتن» تقدیم شده‌ است<br /><br />تاریخ بهنگام رسانی 24/07/1399هجری خورشیدی؛ ا. شربیانی</span>

<div id="review_291532144" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/291532144" />
    <a title="Arthur Graham" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8050734-arthur-graham"><img alt="Arthur Graham" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1511754523p2/8050734.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/291532144?book_show_action=true">Mar 09, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Arthur Graham" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Arthur Graham" href="/user/show/8050734-arthur-graham">Arthur Graham</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer291532144" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7178252862589822551">For newcomers to the world of Charles Bukowski, be forewarned: <i>Pulp</i> is probably not the best place to start.<br/><br/>I say this, not because it doesn’t rank right up there with his other books, or because greener readers will fail to grasp the allusions to earlier work it contains, but rather because as his ultimate novel (completed months before his death) <i>Pulp</i> can easily be seen as Bukowski’s final farewell. In it, the aged author takes his readers on one last foray into familiar territories of sex, ma</span>
  <span id="freeText7178252862589822551" style="display:none">For newcomers to the world of Charles Bukowski, be forewarned: <i>Pulp</i> is probably not the best place to start.<br /><br />I say this, not because it doesn’t rank right up there with his other books, or because greener readers will fail to grasp the allusions to earlier work it contains, but rather because as his ultimate novel (completed months before his death) <i>Pulp</i> can easily be seen as Bukowski’s final farewell. In it, the aged author takes his readers on one last foray into familiar territories of sex, madness, and death, while at the same time expanding on those themes in brilliant and often unexpected ways. Drawing on science fiction and hardboiled noir elements as well, the end result is a bizarre send-up of genre fiction that is just as hilarious and insightful as anything else he wrote.<br /><br />In any case, “farewell” hardly seems the proper way to begin one’s relationship with a writer who spent the better part of five decades compiling such an impressive body of work. Should readers feel obligated to start with Bukowski’s vast catalogue of poems, novels, and short stories if they expect to enjoy<i> Pulp</i>? Certainly not. But since they’d be missing out on some of the best American literature ever written in bypassing these altogether, astute readers would do well to check out a few of his other books as well.<br /><br />But this is supposed to be a review of <i>Pulp</i>, now isn’t it?<br /><br />The plot follows the convoluted capers of one Nick Belane, a down on his luck private eye who somehow manages to stumble upon the case of the century. Well, more like a handful of cases – each of them equally obtuse, and none of them leading anywhere but ever deeper into a darkly comic existential nightmare. Initially retained by Lady Death (a literal femme fatale) to track down the presumably long-dead French novelist, Céline, the plot thickens as Belane is also hired by a husband who suspects his wife of cheating, a mortician in search of a body-snatching space alien, and yet another client who asks him to find someone or something known only as the “Red Sparrow.”<br /><br />Plot-wise that’s about all you really need to know about <i>Pulp</i>, but if plot and character development are what you’re after in a book, then this one probably isn’t for you. Many times throughout his career Bukowski was quoted as saying “genius might be the ability to say a profound thing in a simple way.” This austere philosophy of writing is perhaps most succinctly put by the pithy epitaph adorning his tombstone: DON’T TRY. Whether Bukowski was a genius himself, or whether his final piece of advice should apply to all writers – these are subjects for another time. The point here is that, like Hemingway at his best, Bukowski managed to provoke a breadth and depth of intellectual and emotional responses in his work using only a sparse economy of words and dialogue, and <i>Pulp</i> is no exception.<br /><br />A large portion of Bukowski’s writing had a satirical bent to it, and though it would be a simplification to label him a satirist outright, there can be no denying that much of his fiction contains a strong element of lampoon. Whether denigrating himself or poking fun at such sacrosanct notions as god, country, and anything else associated with the American herd mentality, one cannot read Bukowski and come away without noticing the inordinate amount of tongue in his cheek. In <i>Pulp</i>, Bukowski sets his sights on writing itself – specifically, the more contrived conventions of genre writing.<br /><br />It is no coincidence, after all, that <i>Pulp</i> is called what it’s called (the title being a reference to tawdry dime novels of times past) or that the book itself is dedicated “to bad writing.” Still, if there is any writer capable of taking something bad and making it so much worse that it ends up being good, it is Bukowski. Consider the following excerpt from chapter nine:<br /><br />***<br /><br />I had to straighten out the Celine matter and find the Red Sparrow and here was this flabby ball of flesh worried because his wife was screwing somebody.<br /><br />Then he spoke. “I just want to find out. I just want to find out for myself.”<br /><br />“I don’t come cheap.”<br /><br />“How much”?<br /><br />“6 bucks an hour.”<br /><br />“That doesn’t seem like much money.”<br /><br />“Does to me. You got a photo of your wife?”<br /><br />He dug into his wallet, come up with one, handed it to me.<br /><br />I looked at it.<br /><br />“Oh my! Does she really look like this?”<br /><br />“Yes.”<br /><br />“I’m getting a hard-on just looking at this.”<br /><br />“Hey, don’t be a wise guy!”<br /><br />“Oh, sorry… But I’ll have to keep the photo. I’ll return it when I’m finished.”<br /><br />I put it in my wallet.<br /><br />“Is she still living with you?”<br /><br />“Yes.”<br /><br />“And you go to work?”<br /><br />“Yes.”<br /><br />“And then, sometimes, she…”<br /><br />“Yes.”<br /><br />“And what makes you think she…”<br /><br />“Tips, phone calls, voices in my head, her changed behavior, any number of things.”<br /><br />I pushed a notepad toward him.<br /><br />“Put down your address, home and business, phone, home and business. I’ll take it from here. I’ll nail her ass to the wall. I’ll uncover the whole thing.”<br /><br />“What?”<br /><br />“I am accepting this case, Mr. Bass. Upon its fruition you will be informed.”<br /><br />“‘Fruition’?” he asked. “Listen, are you alright?”<br /><br />“I’m straight. How about you?”<br /><br />“Oh yeah, I’m alright.”<br /><br />“Then don’t worry, I’m your man, I’ll nail her ass!”<br /><br />Bass rose slowly from his chair. He moved toward the door, then turned.<br /><br />“Barton recommended you.”<br /><br />“There you go then! Good afternoon, Mr. Bass.”<br /><br />The door closed and he was gone. Good old Barton.<br /><br />I took her photo out of my wallet and sat there looking at it.<br /><br />You bitch, I thought, you bitch.<br /><br />I got up and locked the door, then took the phone off the hook. I sat behind my desk looking at the photo.<br /><br />You bitch, I thought, I’ll nail your ass! Against the wall! No mercy for you! I’ll catch you in the act! I’ll catch you at it! You whore, you bitch, you whore!<br /><br />I began breathing heavily. I unzipped. Then the earthquake hit. I dropped the photo and ducked under the desk. It was a good one. Around a 6. Felt like it lasted a couple of minutes. Then it stopped. I crawled out from under the desk, still unzipped. I found the photo again, put it back in my wallet, zipped up. Sex was a trap, a snare. It was for animals. I had too much sense for that kind of crap. I put the phone back on the hook, opened the door, stepped out, locked it and walked down to the elevator. I had work to do. I was the best dick in L.A. and Hollywood. I hit the button and waited for the fucking elevator to come on up.<br /><br />***<br /><br />A bit juvenile for the writing of a 73-year-old man? Perhaps. But what Bukowski is up to in this book is not very different from what he’s been up to in all the rest – painting an unapologetic portrait of people as they are (often at their worst), their absurd and futile lives rendered in full view with frank realism and, usually, great humor. Bukowski laughs at the ineffectual, masturbating detective because he really does deserve to be laughed at. And yet Belane is allowed to continue with some semblance of decency, because if there’s one thing we could all use more of, it’s probably that.<br /><br /><i>Pulp</i> is a book that will make fans of sci-fi and detective genre writing wonder what might have been, had Bukowski decided to produce more in those veins (satirical or otherwise). Still, as evidenced by two previously uncollected stories in the recent <i>Portions from a Wine-Stained Notebook</i> – “The Other” and “The Way it Happened” – Bukowski was never quite so two-dimensional in style and subject matter as his critics would have us believe. <i>Pulp</i> is not exactly his first trip into the realm of private eyes and the paranormal, so it should come as no surprise how readily these seemingly foreign elements are assimilated into his more standard tale of bars, broads, and brawls.<br /><br />Bukowski’s earliest novels, <i>Post Office</i> and <i>Factotum</i>, did much to solidify his reputation as a writer of the streets, his own life closely reflecting that of his tenderhearted tough guy alter ego, Henry Chinaski. Later efforts like <i>Women</i> and <i>Ham on Rye</i> continued this literary development, while at the same time nurturing an increasingly effective autobiographical honesty. With <i>Pulp</i>, we are given the impression that Bukowski could’ve continued writing forever about anything, had Lady Death not finally saw fit to extricate him and his typer from this world for good.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7178252862589822551" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_411218202" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/411218202" />
    <a title="David Schaafsma" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/269235-david-schaafsma"><img alt="David Schaafsma" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1574997804p2/269235.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/411218202?book_show_action=true">Aug 31, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="David Schaafsma" class="user" itemprop="url" name="David Schaafsma" href="/user/show/269235-david-schaafsma">David Schaafsma</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/269235-david-schaafsma?shelf=humor">humor</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/269235-david-schaafsma?shelf=fiction-20th-century">fiction-20th-century</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/269235-david-schaafsma?shelf=mystery-detective-thriller">mystery-detective-thriller</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer411218202" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13767766470757563429">Pulp is Charles Bukowski’s last book. So you get curious about that, a dying man’s last words. Is it the foxhole confession at last for a life-long unapologetic vulgarian? Nah. True to his stolid commitment to self-deprecation, satire of pretentiousness and constant drunkenness, Bukowksi, knowing he has little time left, pens his first non-Henry Chinaski fiction, and dedicates it to “bad writing.” His target here is noir fiction, ala Mickey Spillane, with Bukowski’s version named Nicky Belane. I</span>
  <span id="freeText13767766470757563429" style="display:none">Pulp is Charles Bukowski’s last book. So you get curious about that, a dying man’s last words. Is it the foxhole confession at last for a life-long unapologetic vulgarian? Nah. True to his stolid commitment to self-deprecation, satire of pretentiousness and constant drunkenness, Bukowksi, knowing he has little time left, pens his first non-Henry Chinaski fiction, and dedicates it to “bad writing.” His target here is noir fiction, ala Mickey Spillane, with Bukowski’s version named Nicky Belane. It’s a wild often hilarious mishmash of satire of pseudo-existentialist crime fiction--"We are all born to die. We are all born to live"--where Bellane searches for a Red Sparrow (as in Maltese Falcon) and avoids Lady Death. The idea, as it is so many detective stories, is that the detective is searching for clues to a mystery as he searches for the mystery of his own existence. <br /><br />This mystery idea always has had some interesting thematic potential. It actually describes some of the work of Nobel Prize winning writer Patric Modiano (i.e., Missing Person), who uses this theme with serious intent, and successfully. Bukowski, isn't disinterested in the relationship between his work as a writer and his mortality, but he mostly plays the theme for laughs here through detective Belane.<br /><br />“It wasn’t my day. My week. My month. My year. My life. God damn it.”<br /><br />“I'm not dead yet, just in a state of rapid decay, who isn't?” <br /><br />“I gave him my code name. 'This is Mr. Slow Death.’” <br /><br />There are literary tributes to his favorite writers, Celine and Fante, and plenty of booze and broads and bad jokes, natch. And space aliens instead of angels.<br /><br />This should not be the first or only Bukowski you read, and it is not his best work, but my basic three star rating of this book adds a star because of the laugh-out load humor he faces death with. I like and admire that.<br /><br />There’s a bunch of good reviews, but I like this one a lot, from Arthur. I was going to just cut and paste some of what he quotes from the book, but wth, here’s the review: <br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/291532144?book_show_action=true&amp;from_review_page=1">https://www.goodreads.com/review/show...</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="13767766470757563429" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_794242951" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/794242951" />
    <a title="Steven Godin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/26706841-steven-godin"><img alt="Steven Godin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1605181037p2/26706841.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/794242951?book_show_action=true">Dec 19, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Steven Godin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Steven Godin" href="/user/show/26706841-steven-godin">Steven Godin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780753518175" href="/book/show/6883545-pulp">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26706841-steven-godin?shelf=postmodern-fiction">postmodern-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26706841-steven-godin?shelf=america-canada">america-canada</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer794242951" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13367135413607365424">Pulp is the only one of Charles Bukowski’s novels that’s not written from the perspective of Bukowski’s alter ego, Henry Chinaski. After all the agonized and hilarious autobiographical accounts of pain, frustration, poor health and madness of his earlier novels, the great man had at last come to a subject too enormous and painful to deal with directly. This was Bukowski’s last novel, published in 1994, the same year he died of leukemia at the age of 73. As he was writing this book, he knew his d</span>
  <span id="freeText13367135413607365424" style="display:none">Pulp is the only one of Charles Bukowski’s novels that’s not written from the perspective of Bukowski’s alter ego, Henry Chinaski. After all the agonized and hilarious autobiographical accounts of pain, frustration, poor health and madness of his earlier novels, the great man had at last come to a subject too enormous and painful to deal with directly. This was Bukowski’s last novel, published in 1994, the same year he died of leukemia at the age of 73. As he was writing this book, he knew his days were numbered, which saddens me, as would loved to have seen him go out in blaze of filthy glory with something similar to 'Women' or 'Factotum'. Just with a vulgar and laugh-out-nature that goes into overdrive, one final swansong that says 'fuck you'. After all this was what he will always be remembered for. Not for trying to be Chandler. <br /><br />Bukowski’s final novel is written as an allegory. It’s a parody of the hard-boiled detective genre, with Bukowski’s hero/narrator named “Nicky Belane.” Belane spends most of the book looking for a mysterious “Red Sparrow,” a thinly-veiled reference to Dashiell Hammett’s Maltese Falcon, but also a shout-out to his publisher, Black Sparrow Press, which saved him from his hated job at the Post Office and first financed his writing full-time. Belane’s first client, Lady Death, hires him to find the long-deceased novelist Celine, one of Bukowski’s role models as a writer.<br /><br />Although many moments in Pulp are pretty damn funny, the fact there is no Hank just left me feeling a bit empty. The characters we encounter could have been taken from any of the old classic detective novels, so there is nothing really new here. Belane’s innermost thoughts, recurring throughout the novel, are focused on Bukowski’s feelings about his own imminent death, and his struggle to make sense of the inevitable, this was hard going for me, and I admit to shedding a tear, feeling sad about Bukowski distracted me from the plot.<br /><br />But fear not!, Second reading things clicked better, and I just went with it, that's what Charlie would have have wanted.(reading partly in a bar helped!).<br />Bukowski is probably up there now, drunk, in a slumber, and urinating over the world below, hope the lord can forgive him, if not, bollocks then!.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13367135413607365424" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2310311576?book_show_action=true">Aug 02, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mutasim Billah " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mutasim Billah " href="/user/show/20438238-mutasim-billah">Mutasim Billah </a>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/13289436-pulp">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2310311576" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15406191345347600105"><blockquote>
  <i>“It wasn’t my day. My week. My month. My year. My life. God damn it.”</i>
</blockquote><br/><br/>Filthy, sleazy, surreal and hilarious: Bukowski's final completed novel, <i>Pulp</i> is a pulp-fiction parody novel which centers on themes of death and betrayal. The story follows the perspective of Nicky Belane, a hard-boiled, old-timer detective in L.A. trying to solve several strange, surreal interconnected cases by waiting them out.<br/><br/><blockquote>
  <i>“I'm not dead yet, just in a state of rapid decay, who isn't?”</i>
</blockquote><br/><br/>Much of the writing is Bukows</span>
  <span id="freeText15406191345347600105" style="display:none"><blockquote>
  <i>“It wasn’t my day. My week. My month. My year. My life. God damn it.”</i>
</blockquote><br><br>Filthy, sleazy, surreal and hilarious: Bukowski's final completed novel, <i>Pulp</i> is a pulp-fiction parody novel which centers on themes of death and betrayal. The story follows the perspective of Nicky Belane, a hard-boiled, old-timer detective in L.A. trying to solve several strange, surreal interconnected cases by waiting them out.<br><br><blockquote>
  <i>“I'm not dead yet, just in a state of rapid decay, who isn't?”</i>
</blockquote><br><br>Much of the writing is Bukowski musing about mortality and there are several metaphors signifying death. Bukowski dedicates the story to "bad writing", as Bukowski did not plan his mystery novel well and frequently wrote Nicky Belane into holes from which he could not escape. Bukowski wrote some of his most violent, cynical, sarcastic, and shocking work during the final months of his life. Many critics have agreed this novel exemplifies Bukowski showing an acceptance of his own pending mortality.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1533443208i/26078833._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>The story also makes multiple references to men of literature across the ages.<br><br>-&gt; The name of character Nicky Belane rhymes suggestively with the name of author Mickey Spillane.<br><br>-&gt; The <i>Red Sparrow</i> is a spoof of the Black Sparrow Press, owned by John Martin, who is parodied as John Barton in the novel.<br><br>-&gt; The story mentions William Faulkners' <i>As I Lay Dying</i> multiple times. There is also a character signifying French author <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Louis-Ferdinand_C%C3%A9line" rel="nofollow noopener">Céline</a>.<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1533443123ra/26078830.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"></span>

<div id="review_331885073" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/331885073" />
    <a title="Adam Floridia" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1024945-adam-floridia"><img alt="Adam Floridia" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1589128676p2/1024945.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/331885073?book_show_action=true">May 17, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Adam Floridia" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Adam Floridia" href="/user/show/1024945-adam-floridia">Adam Floridia</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer331885073" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6877761330901969479">"'This whole thing is a bad senseless dream,' I said" (200).<br/><br/>That about sums it up; except it isn't bad. It's not good either. I felt like Bukowski was just playing a joke on his readers the whole time, like "hey, let me just write some outlandish fiction with an absurd, nonsensical plot and someone will publish it and lots of people will read it. And I'm going to do that because I can." And he did. And he makes writing seem so easy. And this makes me think that I could write a book like this: si</span>
  <span id="freeText6877761330901969479" style="display:none">"'This whole thing is a bad senseless dream,' I said" (200).<br /><br />That about sums it up; except it isn't bad. It's not good either. I felt like Bukowski was just playing a joke on his readers the whole time, like "hey, let me just write some outlandish fiction with an absurd, nonsensical plot and someone will publish it and lots of people will read it. And I'm going to do that because I can." And he did. And he makes writing seem so easy. And this makes me think that I could write a book like this: simple sentences, cliched dialogue, random plot, poorly developed characters. And I know I can't (<i>yet, he tells himself optimistically</i>), which is one of the reasons I liked this book--he makes it seem easy. I could read this cover to cover without getting bored by the prosaic writing or the trite characters (Death, aliens, Louis Ferdinand-Celine, a more than a few cartoon thugs). And it's because Bukowski somehow makes all that crap palatable.</span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1674926941" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1674926941" />
    <a title="Sam Quixote" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5759543-sam-quixote"><img alt="Sam Quixote" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1529183438p2/5759543.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1674926941?book_show_action=true">Jun 21, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sam Quixote" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sam Quixote" href="/user/show/5759543-sam-quixote">Sam Quixote</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780753511893" href="/book/show/609744.Pulp">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1674926941" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15841968377860265715">Nicky Belane is the best private dick in LA and that makes him a busy boy. He must find the acclaimed, long-presumed dead French novelist Celine for Lady Death, the ultimate femme fatale, help a mortician escape a body-snatching alien called Jeannie Nitro and find the Red Sparrow, whatever that is! But has Belane bitten off more than he can chew this time? <br/><br/>Charles Bukowski’s last book, Pulp, is a helluva way to go out and an odd one too given that his best known works - Post Office, Factotum, Wo</span>
  <span id="freeText15841968377860265715" style="display:none">Nicky Belane is the best private dick in LA and that makes him a busy boy. He must find the acclaimed, long-presumed dead French novelist Celine for Lady Death, the ultimate femme fatale, help a mortician escape a body-snatching alien called Jeannie Nitro and find the Red Sparrow, whatever that is! But has Belane bitten off more than he can chew this time? <br /><br />Charles Bukowski’s last book, Pulp, is a helluva way to go out and an odd one too given that his best known works - Post Office, Factotum, Women and Ham on Rye - were thinly veiled autobiography while Pulp is pure fiction. But it’s a fantastic novel full of Bukowski’s signature wit and world weariness wrapped up in a swiftly-moving plot and fast-talking characters - re-reading it well over a decade after my first time, it remains outstanding. <br /><br />That said, if you’ve never read Buk before I wouldn’t recommend Pulp as the best place to start mostly because it feels like a summary of his career. The references abound: his literary alter-ego Henry Chinaski makes a brief cameo, a hitman called Fante appears while Belane’s looking for Celine (John Fante and Celine were Bukowski’s biggest literary influences - notice the nod to pulp writer Mickey Spillane with the main character’s name Nicky Belane too), and the Red Sparrow could be a reference to Black Sparrow Press, Bukowski’s long-time publisher. <br /><br />Belane is a lot like Chinaski in that he spends a lot of time in bars having verbal fights with its barflies before starting physical ones. He’s a misanthropic old bastard but Buk’s snappy dialogue makes him very entertaining company while also maintaining the noir feel of the narrative. It’s often very funny too with Belane claiming to be expensive (“Six bucks an hour”) and trying to catch a cheating spouse on camera by clownishly chasing them yelling the repeated refrain “I’m gonna nail your ass!!”<br /><br />Buk came from the Hemingway school of spare writing so descriptions are minimal with a heavy emphasis on dialogue but at this point in his life he was so accomplished the reader is easily able to discern the characters from their speech alone (though it’s fair to say they all come off as noir caricatures). Still, Buk was far more playful than Hemingway which is why I like him so much better than tedious old pseudo-macho Papa. He even toys with different genres mixing in some Vonnegut-esque sci-fi to spice things up - and it works! <br /><br />More often than not writers lose their edge as they get older and their later work usually doesn’t stand up to their earlier, more famous novels - Bukowski was the exception and Pulp shows an old pro as sharp as he ever was, at the height of his powers. But, like she did for many of the characters in this book, Lady Death came for Buk too, mere months after he completed this. <br /><br />Pulp is representative of Bukowski’s best work in being raw, clever, original, funny, accessible and superbly written. The dedication says “To bad writing” which, like the title, is a nod to pulp fiction, the irony being that many of those writers are today looked upon as masters - Bukowski included. This book’s also an excellent addition to the noir genre, sliding easily amongst books by greats like Raymond Chandler and Horace McCoy. <br /><br />Those familiar with Bukowski’s work will appreciate Pulp more but if you’re a fan of noir fiction there’s no harm in jumping straight in with this novel. This remains one of my favourite books by one of my favourite writers - Pulp is a sparkling coda for a fine writer.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15841968377860265715" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_782045338" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/782045338" />
    <a title="Barry Wynn" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1808555-barry-wynn"><img alt="Barry Wynn" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1236983799p2/1808555.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/782045338?book_show_action=true">Dec 05, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Barry Wynn" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Barry Wynn" href="/user/show/1808555-barry-wynn">Barry Wynn</a>
      </span>

        rated it

<div id="review_2903666515" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2903666515" />
    <a title="André" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7215128-andr"><img alt="André" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1586543083p2/7215128.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2903666515?book_show_action=true">Jul 26, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="André" class="user" itemprop="url" name="André" href="/user/show/7215128-andr">André</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780753518175" href="/book/show/6883545-pulp">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7215128-andr?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7215128-andr?shelf=novels">novels</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7215128-andr?shelf=literature">literature</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2903666515" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10288661423736844122">Pulp is Bukowski's last opera. It's a meta-pulp fiction that parodies the classic detective stories. This time, our dearest writer, creates a new alter-ego, Nicky Belane, a hard-boiled private detective. Belane is the grimiest side of Bukowski: more rebel, violent and colder than Chinaski, but with a slightly psychopathic behaviour. <br/>The plot follows the absurdity of Belane's daily life. He's a deadbeat character who "solves" cases for a living (or tries to). <br/>Bukowski's alter-ego, in this tale, s</span>
  <span id="freeText10288661423736844122" style="display:none">Pulp is Bukowski's last opera. It's a meta-pulp fiction that parodies the classic detective stories. This time, our dearest writer, creates a new alter-ego, Nicky Belane, a hard-boiled private detective. Belane is the grimiest side of Bukowski: more rebel, violent and colder than Chinaski, but with a slightly psychopathic behaviour. <br />The plot follows the absurdity of Belane's daily life. He's a deadbeat character who "solves" cases for a living (or tries to). <br />Bukowski's alter-ego, in this tale, sees himself in endless holes which he cannot escape. These holes (Belane's absurd situations) reach ironic events that question what is real and what is fantasy. Furthermore, the characters in this story are mysterious, empty and simple. <br />Pulp's cynical features highlight perfectly Bukowski's swansong. It's his rough way to say "fuck ya", to Illness and to death. <br />Pulp (fiction) reaches another level of irony. It's vicious, brutal and disturbing. However, it's a sort of rugged story that few will appreciate. But that's how life can be sometimes, only few can really appreciate and live it.<br /><br />Rating: 4/5 stars</span>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1967053643" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1967053643" />
    <a title="Michael Jandrok" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/66672731-michael-jandrok"><img alt="Michael Jandrok" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1561055227p2/66672731.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1967053643?book_show_action=true">Apr 09, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Michael Jandrok" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Michael Jandrok" href="/user/show/66672731-michael-jandrok">Michael Jandrok</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1967053643" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6643016606734866615">Charles Bukowski's final novel is a swirling mess of genres that aspires to satire, but more often comes across as pastiche. The fact that Bukowski is often lampooning his own style is what makes it so outrageously fun. It is truly what it appears to be, a nod to the pulp novels of the past, and an exercise in bad writing for the sake of bad writing.<br/><br/>I would agree with a few other reviewers, this is NOT the place to start with Bukowski. Stick with "Ham on Rye" or "Post Office" if you need an intr</span>
  <span id="freeText6643016606734866615" style="display:none">Charles Bukowski's final novel is a swirling mess of genres that aspires to satire, but more often comes across as pastiche. The fact that Bukowski is often lampooning his own style is what makes it so outrageously fun. It is truly what it appears to be, a nod to the pulp novels of the past, and an exercise in bad writing for the sake of bad writing.<br /><br />I would agree with a few other reviewers, this is NOT the place to start with Bukowski. Stick with "Ham on Rye" or "Post Office" if you need an introduction to the man and his unique style. This particular work was his final offering, as canny a recognition of ones own mortality as you will ever find on a bookshelf. The cast of characters is typical Bukowski, from the often befuddled PI Nick Belane to his seedy landlord and his thrusting, undulating femme fatales. You can almost taste the cheap whisky and smell the cigarette smoke coming off of each page. It's part detective send up, part science fiction, part comedy, and finally part refection on the often obtuse nature of life itself. Bukowski knew he didn't have long to live when he wrote this, and the existential fear of death pervades the final notes of the book. It's like going into that tunnel and seeing the light, only to realize that it's an oncoming train.<br /><br />Why only 4 stars? Because at the end of the day it just didn't hold up to the man's other works. It was kinda thin, kinda reedy, definitely not the work of an author at the height of his powers. That said, it's still a powerful work. Bukowski disturbs me like no other author, and the end of this book stayed with me a while. <br /><br />You definitely need it in your library if you are a Bukowski fan. Just don't judge the man on this work alone. Go find him in his monstrous prime, when he was spinning tales of lowlife danger instead of wallowing in the desperation of a magnificent life bearing down upon its final days.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6643016606734866615" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2229809812" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2229809812" />
    <a title="Tim" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7202176-tim"><img alt="Tim" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1324164999p2/7202176.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2229809812?book_show_action=true">Apr 11, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tim" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tim" href="/user/show/7202176-tim">Tim</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7202176-tim?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7202176-tim?shelf=1990s">1990s</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2229809812" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18396770567504009618">What do a literal femme fatale (appropriately named Lady Death), the French author Louis-Ferdinand Céline, a husband with a maybe cheating wife and space aliens have in common? Hell if I know, but Charles Bukowski obviously felt like they all fit together, as they all tie into the cases featured in his final novel Pulp.<br/><br/>Pulp is many things. It is a send up of noir style mysteries, with a narrator even named Nicky Belane who has to search for the illusive Red Sparrow. The writing is purposely bad </span>
  <span id="freeText18396770567504009618" style="display:none">What do a literal femme fatale (appropriately named Lady Death), the French author Louis-Ferdinand Céline, a husband with a maybe cheating wife and space aliens have in common? Hell if I know, but Charles Bukowski obviously felt like they all fit together, as they all tie into the cases featured in his final novel Pulp.<br /><br />Pulp is many things. It is a send up of noir style mysteries, with a narrator even named Nicky Belane who has to search for the illusive Red Sparrow. The writing is purposely bad in some places and if you listen closely you can practically hear Bukowski’s ghost giggling. On a more serious note, the book is also about Bukowski coming to terms with his own death. He found out when writing that he didn’t have long to live and the book was published a few months before his death. We get many such nods to this with Bukowski trying to face this with a sense of humor; for example his narrator goes by the name Mr. Slow Death at one point. There are various quotes I could point to that have a humorous, but melancholy air. Here are a few of my favorites:<br /><br />“I had to think. I tried to think. The fly was still crawling along the desk. I rolled up the Racing Form, took a swat at it and missed. It wasn’t my day. My week. My month. My year. My life.”<br /><br />“You only live once, right? Well, except for Lazarus. Poor sucker, he had to die twice.”<br /><br />“I killed four flies while waiting. Damn, death was everywhere. Man, bird, beast, reptile, rodent, insect, fish didn’t have a chance. The fix was in. I didn’t know what to do about it. I got depressed. You know, I see a box boy at the supermarket, he’s packing my groceries, then I see him sticking himself into his own grave along with the toilet paper, the beer and the chicken breasts.”<br /><br />But don’t think the book is all doom and gloom, though it is melancholy, it is extremely funny as well. There are several laugh at loud encounters in the book, as Belane goes from one bizarre encounter to the next, my personal favorite being an encounter at a funeral parlor where he theorizes about the space alien plan… yeah, it’s hard to explain. You’ll have to check it out for yourself.<br /><br />In the end though, I’m a little torn. It is as I said, a very funny novel and I do appreciate from an artistic standpoint that Bukowski decided to go out with this novel which he dedicates to “bad writing.” That said it is a very… random book, with things happening for the hell of it and plot lines crossing themselves out seemingly as the author tires from them. Without the biographical info, I think I would have found much of the book a little senseless, but knowing what he was going through changes so much of how I processed it. Personally I didn’t find this as humorous as “Post Office” but there is a lot that I respect here. A solid 3 out of 5. </span>
  <a data-text-id="18396770567504009618" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3235472850" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3235472850" />
    <a title="James Tingle" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/66386578-james-tingle"><img alt="James Tingle" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1491430223p2/66386578.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3235472850?book_show_action=true">Mar 17, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="James Tingle" class="user" itemprop="url" name="James Tingle" href="/user/show/66386578-james-tingle">James Tingle</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780753511893" href="/book/show/609744.Pulp">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3235472850" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8763866202836088184"><br/>I've read all of Bukowski's novels now and this was the last one that I read and the final book that he wrote, dying not long after its completion. I would have to say that his other novels are better, but this is still pretty darn good and plenty of fun as well. Whereas the other novels are all about Henry Chinaski, his alter-ego, this one has a different protagonist, a Mr Nicky Belane, who is a private detective and a drunken no good one at that! At first, I found it quite odd not reading abou</span>
  <span id="freeText8763866202836088184" style="display:none"><br />I've read all of Bukowski's novels now and this was the last one that I read and the final book that he wrote, dying not long after its completion. I would have to say that his other novels are better, but this is still pretty darn good and plenty of fun as well. Whereas the other novels are all about Henry Chinaski, his alter-ego, this one has a different protagonist, a Mr Nicky Belane, who is a private detective and a drunken no good one at that! At first, I found it quite odd not reading about Chinaski, but soon enough, the old familiar themes come to the surface- drinking, hangovers, loose women, bars and general debauchery and the old familiarity of Bukowski quickly resurfaces, which is what you want of course. The plot is fairly straight forward and has the hard boiled detective thing going on, but with some Bukowski mayhem liberally applied, which gives it quite a surreal edge at times and throws up a few tasty surprises. You have your tense, guarded conversations in phone booths and various leads to follow up on and mysterious, sultry women playing mind games with Detective Belane, but its all turned up to eleven, Spinal Tap style, making it a frenetic and pretty riveting read...<br />I would say if you are new to Bukowski, then don't start with this one as its not totally typical of his other novels, although it has some similar themes...better to read Ham on Rye, Factotum, Post Office and the rest of them first and then finish with this as a kind of indulgent, tasty, Bukowski pudding, at the end of the hearty and nourishing main meal. </span>
  <a data-text-id="8763866202836088184" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_809480411" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/809480411" />
    <a title="Joy (joyous reads)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4352734-joy-joyous-reads"><img alt="Joy (joyous reads)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1526951297p2/4352734.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/809480411?book_show_action=true">Jan 02, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Joy (joyous reads)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Joy (joyous reads)" href="/user/show/4352734-joy-joyous-reads">Joy (joyous reads)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4352734-joy-joyous-reads?shelf=own">own</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer809480411" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15817362084665695248">Yikes. Reading the last book the author's ever written as an introduction to his work is NEVER a good idea. My brain is fried. <br/>_________________________________<br/><br/>The whole time I was reading this, all I could think of was what my brain would look like if it was on meth.<br/><br/>This book should come with a warning: “Book may induce numbness to the brain that may lead to permanent damage.” Or something to that effect.<br/><br/>Clearly, my first foray into Bukowski’s world did not get off to a great start. I read some</span>
  <span id="freeText15817362084665695248" style="display:none">Yikes. Reading the last book the author's ever written as an introduction to his work is NEVER a good idea. My brain is fried. <br />_________________________________<br /><br />The whole time I was reading this, all I could think of was what my brain would look like if it was on meth.<br /><br />This book should come with a warning: “Book may induce numbness to the brain that may lead to permanent damage.” Or something to that effect.<br /><br />Clearly, my first foray into Bukowski’s world did not get off to a great start. I read somewhere that this was his farewell book as he wrote it before he died. So being one of the greatest minds in the modern literature, of course the book was full of metaphors for the life he’d lived that I, unfortunately, knew nothing about. This is what happens when I become an overzealous reader. It’s like face-planting in front of a room-full of people with my skirt pulled up, thereby showing them a piece of my caboose.<br /><br />It’s humiliating.<br /><br />Anyway, I bought a couple of Bukowski’s short novels a while back because I wanted a little glimpse into how his mind works. Incidentally, this one caught my eye because I’m such a whore for pulp fiction covers. Last weekend, while I was trying to figure out what to feature on my throwback review, I thought it was as good a time as any. At 208 pages, and with chapters that sometimes are only a paragraph or two long, Pulp was just perfect.<br /><br />Big mistake. Huge.<br /><br />Imagine the most misogynistic character you’ve ever known then multiply that by ten. Dress him up as someone who drinks himself to stupor, gambles money he doesn’t have, jerks off at a woman’s voice over the phone, then makes deals with people only to double cross them in the end.<br /><br />Are you imagining this person?<br /><br />If you are, then congratulations. You just found yourself face to face with Nick Belane, PI. This man is quite the character. He’d often found himself in situations of his own making. It would be funny if it wasn’t so terrible. Anyway, his assignment to find Céline spear headed a domino effect of troubles for him. From one client to another, he crosses and double crosses each one. It was exhausting. So exhausting that I wished someone would put the poor sob out of his misery by the next page.<br /><br />I went and read a little more about this book’s history and I found out that this book was sort of Bukowski’s “fuck you” to the world. He made fun of pulp fiction, and fired shots at some authors. I really am clueless. It wasn’t a good idea to read the last work that the author penned as he was close to dying. There was a deliberate referral to his pending escape as one of the characters is called, Lady Death. The one looking for the author Celine, who incidentally was supposed to be dead as he was born in the 1800s.<br /><br />Needless to say, I think Bukowski’s brilliance lay waste on someone like me.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15817362084665695248" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_14275719" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/14275719" />
    <a title="Travis" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/864099-travis"><img alt="Travis" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/14275719?book_show_action=true">Feb 01, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Travis" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Travis" href="/user/show/864099-travis">Travis</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer14275719" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6776549601229837232">Take away a noir detective's story, his class, his street smarts, and write an entire book without ever giving him a reason to live. Welcome to Nick Belane's world in "Pulp." <br/><br/>This is crime fiction that is just too real and too surreal at the same time. Do we ever really believe that someone can smoke a pack of cigerettes and drink a pint of whiskey every day and still look good enough to score a smoking hot femme fatale? We don't, and Burkowski never let's us pretend that Belane ever could. Bela</span>
  <span id="freeText6776549601229837232" style="display:none">Take away a noir detective's story, his class, his street smarts, and write an entire book without ever giving him a reason to live. Welcome to Nick Belane's world in "Pulp." <br /><br />This is crime fiction that is just too real and too surreal at the same time. Do we ever really believe that someone can smoke a pack of cigerettes and drink a pint of whiskey every day and still look good enough to score a smoking hot femme fatale? We don't, and Burkowski never let's us pretend that Belane ever could. Belane is 50 going on 70, so burned out he has dark charred wrinkles and can't even keep his right hand off his penis when talking to a woman over the phone. "Pulp" meshes several classic noir set-ups together, but never adds that last spice of "gritty detective with a heart of gold." And at the end of "Pulp" we realize this is how noir should be played out. This is the ending that Spade and Marlowe both would have gotten if reality had sunk in to their stories a little deeper. <br /><br />This book can be a longer read - it includes a chapter describing Belane celebrating his "progress" on multiple cases by trolling Sunset and getting into bar fights for no reason. The chapters are very short, which adds to a little confusion if you are trying to following events closer then Belane is. <br /><br />I think this book is ultimately a giant middle finger to Hollywood detectives. A great read and a lot funnier the second time around. </span>
  <a data-text-id="6776549601229837232" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText1868161779205346308" style="display:none">PULP is spectacular. I am still thinking about it, I think PULP could be nearly as good as FACTOTUM and it sort of outguns HAM ON RYE, POST OFFICE, WOMEN and HOLLYWOOD.<br /><br />Bukowski is in the form of his life here. The humor is brilliant. The social commentary is sharp. And it also has the two most depressing pages (chapter no.22 in the Ecco; 1st Ecco Ed edition ,May 31, 2002) Bukowski has ever written.<br /><br />It is incredible how Bukowski combines slapstick humor, sharp social commentary and melancholia without any of it coming off as malapropos. he was an incredible craftsman. Just incredibly audacious writing. He just hit the ball out of the park in his last novel. What a wonderful farewell.The social commentary in PULP is among the best Bukowski has ever done. Profound and top of the league stuff.<br /></span>

<div id="review_142595384" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/142595384" />
    <a title="Yair Ben-Zvi" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3223578-yair-ben-zvi"><img alt="Yair Ben-Zvi" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1588137043p2/3223578.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/142595384?book_show_action=true">Jan 21, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Yair Ben-Zvi" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Yair Ben-Zvi" href="/user/show/3223578-yair-ben-zvi">Yair Ben-Zvi</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3223578-yair-ben-zvi?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer142595384" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1998647944037175097">"We have come to terms". Sorry, just wanted to start my review with a nicely terminal quote from a relatively obscure show (the fabulous anime The Big O for those interested) before jumping in. Here we have Bukowski's final published novel and for all its various flaws (and there are quite a few don't get me wrong) the work still succeeds through a rare combination of authorial resignation, acceptance, the tragedy of the mortal human condition, and the inverted triumph that results from all this</span>
  <span id="freeText1998647944037175097" style="display:none">"We have come to terms". Sorry, just wanted to start my review with a nicely terminal quote from a relatively obscure show (the fabulous anime The Big O for those interested) before jumping in. Here we have Bukowski's final published novel and for all its various flaws (and there are quite a few don't get me wrong) the work still succeeds through a rare combination of authorial resignation, acceptance, the tragedy of the mortal human condition, and the inverted triumph that results from all this which can all be discerned in the books dedication "To bad writing".<br /><br />I've mentioned in my past reviews of Bukowski's work that I read him in that period between community college and university, wherein I fantasized about the life he so viscerally put to the page. That life was the writing life. Not just any writing life mind you, but Bukowksi's singular interpretation of this, of the lone writer, cast off, mocked and derided by his fellow man, left sadly but ultimately beautifully to his own devices (and vices, of course) to occasionally cobble together a piece of poetry or prose that would rock the literary world..or his small corner of it at least. And all this while he wasn't slowly killing himself with drugs (mostly alcohol) women (mostly the female equivalents of himself) and just plain apathy and lack of output.<br /><br />I like to think that I've hopefully matured in outlook since then but I can't help but look back on those years and my concomitant reading of Bukowksi during those years with fondness, even affection. I feel like despite his boastful and boisterous (and at times despairing, wrathful, and lachrymose) moods, nobody understood Bukowski better than Bukowski himself. And it's this level of self-cognizance that lends itself so beautifully to the flawed but ultimately fascinating (and hence worth reading) text of Pulp.<br /><br />I won't spoil anything but suffice it to say the book stands decently on its own but even better if you, like me, have made acquainted yourself with Buk's oeuvre, his tropes and hangups, and yes, even his repeated weaknesses in the craft. But Bukowski, maybe more so than any other writer I've yet read, not only understood his shortcomings, he embraced them and, again unlike most writers I've seen up until now, he actually managed a level of writerly evolution and transcendence that goes beyond what the superficially minded might deride as just 'weak' writing. There's more to this book and Bukowksi as an author than that.<br /><br />This idea of Bukowski's is I think best represented with an anecdote I've heard about him. It was regarding a review of one of his then recent collections of poetry that a reviewer had written, maybe facetiously, maybe earnestly, that Bukowksi (then in his 60's I think) was finally 'showing improvement'. Bukowski ackowledged this to his laughing audience and herein, I believe, lies his singular status as a writer, even a thinker.<br /><br />Is it perfect? No. Is it a masterwork? Hell no. And has Bukowski written better work than this? Oh undoubtedly. But where Bukowski succeeds is exactly where few writers, save Kafka, have succeeded, that is, he succeeds in the realm of failure, a realm too few writers and artists are afraid to acknowledge let alone transcend.<br /><br />On Charles Bukowski's grave it reads "Don't Try" and to that I can only say, write, "Damn, I think you were right,".<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="1998647944037175097" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_255436837" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/255436837" />
    <a title="Matt" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2174829-matt"><img alt="Matt" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1238462720p2/2174829.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/255436837?book_show_action=true">Jan 03, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Matt" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Matt" href="/user/show/2174829-matt">Matt</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer255436837" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6857227096784068360">This was a classic private eye novel, with some interesting twists. Similar writing style to Post Office, and another great character. Nick Belane is basically Sam Spade, you know, if Sam Spade were a huge drunk ass hole with a propensity to get in bar fights. Despite seeming to stumble through life drunk and betting on the ponies (very similar to Chinaski), Belane manages to solve pretty much any case that comes to him (especially the ones involving Lady Death and aliens). He solves cases by pi</span>
  <span id="freeText6857227096784068360" style="display:none">This was a classic private eye novel, with some interesting twists. Similar writing style to Post Office, and another great character. Nick Belane is basically Sam Spade, you know, if Sam Spade were a huge drunk ass hole with a propensity to get in bar fights. Despite seeming to stumble through life drunk and betting on the ponies (very similar to Chinaski), Belane manages to solve pretty much any case that comes to him (especially the ones involving Lady Death and aliens). He solves cases by pissing off everyone he encounters, kicking people in the family jewels and roughing them up with brass knuckles. There is some of the greatest dialogue I have ever read in this book. Belane, just doesn't care about anything, is very quick witted and basically a horrible person, and it never gets old. At one point he calls a phone sex line, has a completely inane conversation, which finally ends when the girl hangs up on him because he asks her to wait while he goes pee. Absolutely hilarious scene, probably my favorite. Another great book, and I look forward to reading much more Bukowski.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6857227096784068360" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_63614005" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/63614005" />
    <a title="Garrett Mortin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2359802-garrett-mortin"><img alt="Garrett Mortin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1246035796p2/2359802.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/63614005?book_show_action=true">Jul 15, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Garrett Mortin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Garrett Mortin" href="/user/show/2359802-garrett-mortin">Garrett Mortin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2359802-garrett-mortin?shelf=summer-reading-2009">summer-reading-2009</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer63614005" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1490801451475631803">Pulp is the title of Charles Bukowski's final novel before his death in 1994. As huge Bukowski admire, i was excited to read his take on the detective "pulp" genre. He portrays himself as Private Detective Nick Belane, and we follow him as tries to solve four different cases. Between barroom brawls, and pulls from the bottle, Belane pushes on through a string mistaken identities, and confronts death head on. In my mind anything Bukowski writes is golden, but i will say of all his work this novel</span>
  <span id="freeText1490801451475631803" style="display:none">Pulp is the title of Charles Bukowski's final novel before his death in 1994. As huge Bukowski admire, i was excited to read his take on the detective "pulp" genre. He portrays himself as Private Detective Nick Belane, and we follow him as tries to solve four different cases. Between barroom brawls, and pulls from the bottle, Belane pushes on through a string mistaken identities, and confronts death head on. In my mind anything Bukowski writes is golden, but i will say of all his work this novel left me a bit unfulfilled. Pulp is not for the casual fan, this novel is for those who have drank, sweated, and bled with Bukowski through all his drunken and decayed adventures. I advise you to read Post Office, Ham on Rye, Women, and Factotum before diving into this novel. This is Bukowski's literary exit, his wave goodbye.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1490801451475631803" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('RQ4lF89KNQ40Y9IFUgJiVviGo404xCmRq1mMoM50quZpe4WQ6ZcRi3UC855UGNheepyhoL6rKlegS1fmtWlLdA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('FPuLrR6ai+Zp42d0KXWRAtsFY4PtnWSNfYc+qucVcpw4jisqOEevYyiCRu8vbysKWR9hrmvyZ0t2leXsnAiTDg==')})
  }

It is the tragic monologue of a woman speaking with the child she carries in her womb. This letter confronts the burning theme of abortion, and the meaning of life, by asking difficult questions: Is it fair to impose life even if it means suffering? Would it be better not to be born at all?\nLetter to a Child Never Born touches on the real meaning of being a woman: the power to give life or not. When the book begins, the protagonist is upset after learning she is pregnant. She knows nothing about the child, except that this creature depends totally and uniquely on her own choices. The creation of another person directly within one’s own body is a very shocking thing. The sense of responsibility is huge; it is a heavy burden that gives life to endless reflections, from the origin of our existence to the shame of our selfishness. If the child could choose, would he prefer to be born, to grow up, and to suffer, or would he return to the joyful limbo from which he came?       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/13275.Charles_Bukowski">About Charles Bukowski</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer8196435942159301856"><b>Henry Charles Bukowski</b> (born as <b>Heinrich Karl Bukowski</b>) was a German-born American poet, novelist and short story writer. His writing was influenced by the social, cultural and economic ambience of his home city of Los Angeles.It is marked by an emphasis on the ordinary lives of poor Americans, the act of writing, alcohol, relationships with women and the drudgery of work. Bukowski wrote thousands</span>
  <span id="freeText8196435942159301856" style="display:none"><b>Henry Charles Bukowski</b> (born as <b>Heinrich Karl Bukowski</b>) was a German-born American poet, novelist and short story writer. His writing was influenced by the social, cultural and economic ambience of his home city of Los Angeles.It is marked by an emphasis on the ordinary lives of poor Americans, the act of writing, alcohol, relationships with women and the drudgery of work. Bukowski wrote thousands of poems, hundreds of short stories and six novels, eventually publishing over sixty books<br /><br />Charles Bukowski was the only child of an American soldier and a German mother. At the age of three, he came with his family to the United States and grew up in Los Angeles. He attended Los Angeles City College from 1939 to 1941, then left school and moved to New York City to become a writer. His lack of publishing success at this time caused him to give up writing in 1946 and spurred a ten-year stint of heavy drinking. After he developed a bleeding ulcer, he decided to take up writing again. He worked a wide range of jobs to support his writing, including dishwasher, truck driver and loader, mail carrier, guard, gas station attendant, stock boy, warehouse worker, shipping clerk, post office clerk, parking lot attendant, Red Cross orderly, and elevator operator. He also worked in a dog biscuit factory, a slaughterhouse, a cake and cookie factory, and he hung posters in New York City subways.<br /><br />Bukowski published his first story when he was twenty-four and began writing poetry at the age of thirty-five. His first book of poetry was published in 1959; he went on to publish more than forty-five books of poetry and prose, including <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/141526.Pulp" rel="nofollow noopener">Pulp</a> (1994), <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/50463.Screams_from_the_Balcony" rel="nofollow noopener">Screams from the Balcony</a> (1993), and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/70884.The_Last_Night_of_the_Earth_Poems" rel="nofollow noopener">The Last Night of the Earth Poems</a> (1992).<br /><br />He died of leukemia in San Pedro on March 9, 1994.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8196435942159301856" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

            <span class="readable">&ldquo;We waited and waited. All of us. Didn't the shrink know that waiting was one of the things that drove people crazy? People waited all their lives. They waited to live, they waited to die. They waited in line to buy toilet paper. They waited in line for money. And if they didn't have any money they waited in longer lines. You waited to go to sleep and then you waited to awaken. You waited to get married and you waited to get divorced. You waited for it to rain, you waited for it to stop. You waited to eat and then you waited to eat again. You waited in a shrink's office with a bunch of psychos and you wondered if you were one.&rdquo;</span>
